In it you maintain that the earth moves; that the sun occupies the lowest, and thus the central, place in the universe... Therefore with the utmost earnestness I entreat you, most learned sir, unless I inconvenience you, to communicate this discovery of yours to scholars, and at the earliest possible moment to send me your writings on the sphere of the universe together with the tables and whatever else you have that is relevant to this subject ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webexhibits_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-webexhibits-72">[57]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>By then Copernicus's work was nearing its definitive form, and rumors about his theory had reached educated people all over Europe. Despite urgings from many quarters, Copernicus delayed publication of his book, perhaps from fear of criticism—a fear delicately expressed in the subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dedication_to_Pope_Paul_III" title="Dedication to Pope Paul III">dedication</a> of his masterpiece to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>. Scholars disagree on whether Copernicus's concern was limited to possible astronomical and philosophical objections, or whether he was also concerned about religious objections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[p]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium"><i>De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</i></span></h3>
<p>Copernicus was still working on <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (even if not certain that he wanted to publish it) when in 1539 <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Georg Joachim Rheticus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a> mathematician, arrived in Frombork. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a>, a close theological ally of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, had arranged for Rheticus to visit several astronomers and study with them. Rheticus became Copernicus's pupil, staying with him for two years and writing a book, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narratio_prima" title="Narratio prima">Narratio prima</a></i> (First Account), outlining the essence of Copernicus's theory. In 1542 Rheticus published a treatise on <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a> by Copernicus (later included as chapters 13 and 14 of Book I of <i>De revolutionibus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[58]</a></sup>
Under strong pressure from Rheticus, and having seen the favorable first general reception of his work, Copernicus finally agreed to give <i>De revolutionibus</i> to his close friend, <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a>, bishop of <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno" title="Chełmno">Chełmno</a> (Kulm), to be delivered to Rheticus for printing by the German printer <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Petreius" title="Johannes Petreius">Johannes Petreius</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> (<i>Nürnberg</i>), Germany. While Rheticus initially supervised the printing, he had to leave Nuremberg before it was completed, and he handed over the task of supervising the rest of the printing to a Lutheran theologian, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Osiander" title="Andreas Osiander">Andreas Osiander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive-75">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Osiander added an unauthorised and unsigned preface, defending Copernicus's work against those who might be offended by its novel hypotheses. He argued that "different hypotheses are sometimes offered for one and the same motion [and therefore] the astronomer will take as his first choice that hypothesis which is the easiest to grasp." According to Osiander, "these hypotheses need not be true nor even probable. [I]f they provide a calculus consistent with the observations, that alone is enough."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1561" data-file-width="907" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG/170px-Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG/255px-Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG/340px-Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicolaus_Copernicus_epitaph.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1735 <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">epitaph</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frombork_Cathedral" title="Frombork Cathedral">Frombork Cathedral</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3333" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/220px-Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/330px-Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/440px-Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frombork Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p>Toward the close of 1542, Copernicus was seized with <a href="/wiki/Apoplexy" title="Apoplexy">apoplexy</a> and paralysis, and he died at age 70 on 24 May 1543. Legend has it that he was presented with the final printed pages of his <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">Dē revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> on the very day that he died, allowing him to take farewell of his life's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[q]</a></sup> He is reputed to have awoken from a stroke-induced coma, looked at his book, and then died peacefully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[r]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copernicus was reportedly buried in Frombork Cathedral, where a 1580 epitaph stood until being defaced; it was replaced in 1735. For over two centuries, archaeologists searched the cathedral in vain for Copernicus's remains. Efforts to locate them in 1802, 1909, 1939 had come to nought. In 2004 a team led by Jerzy Gąssowski, head of an <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> institute in <a href="/wiki/Pu%C5%82tusk" title="Pułtusk">Pułtusk</a>, began a new search, guided by the research of historian <a href="/wiki/Jerzy_Sikorski" title="Jerzy Sikorski">Jerzy Sikorski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gass2005_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-gass2005-79">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetic_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic-80">[62]</a></sup> In August 2005, after scanning beneath the cathedral floor, they discovered what they believed to be Copernicus's remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polish_tests_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polish_tests-81">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery was announced only after further research, on 3 November 2008. Gąssowski said he was "almost 100 percent sure it is Copernicus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100_Percent_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-100_Percent-82">[64]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensic" title="Forensic">Forensic</a> expert Capt. Dariusz Zajdel of the Polish Police Central Forensic Laboratory used the skull to reconstruct a face that closely resembled the features—including a broken nose and a scar above the left eye—on a Copernicus self-portrait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100_Percent_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-100_Percent-82">[64]</a></sup> The expert also determined that the skull belonged to a man who had died around age 70—Copernicus's age at the time of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polish_tests_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polish_tests-81">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The grave was in poor condition, and not all the remains of the skeleton were found; missing, among other things, was the lower jaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-83">[65]</a></sup> The DNA from the bones found in the grave matched hair samples taken from a book owned by Copernicus which was kept at the library of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Uppsala" title="University of Uppsala">University of Uppsala</a> in Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetic_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genetic-80">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gravemystery_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-gravemystery-84">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 May 2010 Copernicus was given a second funeral in a <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a> led by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Kowalczyk" title="Józef Kowalczyk">Józef Kowalczyk</a>, the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papal_nuncio" title="Papal nuncio">papal nuncio</a> to Poland and newly named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primate_of_Poland" title="Primate of Poland">Primate of Poland</a>. Copernicus's remains were reburied in the same spot in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frombork_Cathedral" title="Frombork Cathedral">Frombork Cathedral</a> where part of his skull and other bones had been found. A black granite tombstone now identifies him as the founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> and also a church <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canon_(priest)" title="Canon (priest)">canon</a>. The tombstone bears a representation of Copernicus's model of the Solar System—a golden Sun encircled by six of the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD20100525_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-PD20100525-85">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Copernican_system">Copernican system</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican heliocentrism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessors">Predecessors</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philolaus" title="Philolaus">Philolaus</a> (c. 470 – c. 385 BCE) described an astronomical system in which a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Fire" title="Central Fire">Central Fire</a> (different from the Sun) occupied the centre of the universe, and a counter-Earth, the Earth, Moon, the Sun itself, planets, and stars all revolved around it, in that order outward from the centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive39_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive39-86">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heraclides_Ponticus" title="Heraclides Ponticus">Heraclides Ponticus</a> (387–312 BCE) proposed that the Earth rotates on its axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive40_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive40-87">[69]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> (c. 310 BCE – c. 230 BCE) was the first to advance a theory that the earth orbited the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive38_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive38-88">[70]</a></sup> Further mathematical details of Aristarchus's heliocentric system were worked out around 150 BCE by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia" title="Seleucus of Seleucia">Seleucus of Seleucia</a>. Though Aristarchus's original text has been lost, a reference in <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>' book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></i> (<i>Archimedis Syracusani Arenarius &amp; Dimensio Circuli</i>) describes a work by Aristarchus in which he advanced the heliocentric model. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Little_Heath" title="Thomas Little Heath">Thomas Heath</a> gives the following English translation of Archimedes's text:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You are now aware ['you' being King Gelon] that the "universe" is the name given by most astronomers to the sphere the centre of which is the centre of the earth, while its radius is equal to the straight line between the centre of the sun and the centre of the earth. This is the common account (τά γραφόμενα) as you have heard from astronomers. But Aristarchus has brought out a <i>book consisting of certain hypotheses</i>, wherein it appears, as a consequence of the assumptions made, that the universe is many times greater than the "universe" just mentioned. His hypotheses are that <i>the fixed stars and the sun remain unmoved, that the earth revolves about the sun on the circumference of a circle, the sun lying in the middle of the orbit</i>, and that the sphere of the fixed stars, situated about the same centre as the sun, is so great that the circle in which he supposes the earth to revolve bears such a proportion to the distance of the fixed stars as the centre of the sphere bears to its surface.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></i></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In an early unpublished manuscript of <i>De Revolutionibus</i> (which still survives), Copernicus mentioned the (non-heliocentric) 'moving Earth' theory of Philolaus and the possibility that Aristarchus also had a 'moving Earth' theory (though it is unlikely that he was aware that it was a heliocentric theory). He removed both references from his final published manuscript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AristarchusNote01a_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-AristarchusNote01a-9">[c]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kish1978_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kish1978-11">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copernicus was probably aware that Pythagoras's system involved a moving Earth. The Pythagorean system was mentioned by Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copernicus owned a copy of Giorgio Valla's <i>De expetendis et fugiendis rebus</i>, which included a translation of Plutarch's reference to Aristarchus's heliostaticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Copernicus's dedication of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_the_Revolutions_of_the_Heavenly_Spheres" title="On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres">On the Revolutions</a></i> to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>—which Copernicus hoped would dampen criticism of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> by "babblers... completely ignorant of [astronomy]"—the book's author wrote that, in rereading all of philosophy, in the pages of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> he had found references to those few thinkers who dared to move the Earth "against the traditional opinion of astronomers and almost against common sense."
</p><p>The prevailing theory during Copernicus's lifetime was the one that <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> published in his <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 150 CE</span>; the Earth was the stationary center of the universe. Stars were embedded in a large outer sphere that rotated rapidly, approximately daily, while each of the planets, the Sun, and the Moon were embedded in their own, smaller spheres. Ptolemy's system employed devices, including <a href="/wiki/Deferent_and_epicycle" title="Deferent and epicycle">epicycles, deferents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equant" title="Equant">equants</a>, to account for observations that the paths of these bodies differed from simple, circular orbits centered on the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Beginning in the 10th century, a tradition criticizing Ptolemy developed within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">Islamic astronomy</a>, which climaxed with <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> of <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>'s <i>Al-Shukūk 'alā Baṭalamiyūs</i> ("Doubts Concerning Ptolemy").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[76]</a></sup> Several Islamic astronomers questioned the Earth's apparent immobility,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ragep-95">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ragep2-96">[78]</a></sup> and centrality within the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Setia_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Setia-97">[79]</a></sup> Some accepted that the earth rotates around its axis, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sa%27id_al-Sijzi" title="Abu Sa'id al-Sijzi">Abu Sa'id al-Sijzi</a> (d. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1020</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-young-99">[81]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">al-Biruni</a>, al-Sijzi invented an <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a> based on a belief held by some of his contemporaries "that the motion we see is due to the Earth's movement and not to that of the sky."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-young-99">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[82]</a></sup> That others besides al-Sijzi held this view is further confirmed by a reference from an Arabic work in the 13th century which states: </p><blockquote><p>According to the geometers [or engineers] (<i>muhandisīn</i>), the earth is in constant circular motion, and what appears to be the motion of the heavens is actually due to the motion of the earth and not the stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-young-99">[81]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_al-Bitruji" title="Nur ad-Din al-Bitruji">Nur ad-Din al-Bitruji</a> proposed a complete alternative to the Ptolemaic system (although not heliocentric).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEA-bitruji_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEA-bitruji-101">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSB_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSB-102">[84]</a></sup> He declared the Ptolemaic system as an imaginary model, successful at predicting planetary positions, but not real or physical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEA-bitruji_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-BEA-bitruji-101">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSB_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSB-102">[84]</a></sup> Al-Bitruji's alternative system spread through most of Europe during the 13th century, with debates and refutations of his ideas continued up to the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSB_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-DSB-102">[84]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tusi-couple.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Tusi-couple.gif/220px-Tusi-couple.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Tusi-couple.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tusi-couple.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mathematical techniques developed in the 13th to 14th centuries by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mo%27ayyeduddin_al-Urdi" title="Mo'ayyeduddin al-Urdi">Mo'ayyeduddin al-Urdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Shatir" title="Ibn al-Shatir">Ibn al-Shatir</a> for geocentric models of planetary motions closely resemble some of those used later by Copernicus in his heliocentric models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[85]</a></sup> Copernicus used what is now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdi_lemma" title="Urdi lemma">Urdi lemma</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a> in the same planetary models as found in Arabic sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saliba95_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba95-104">[86]</a></sup> Furthermore, the exact replacement of the <a href="/wiki/Equant" title="Equant">equant</a> by two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicycles" title="Epicycles">epicycles</a> used by Copernicus in the <i><a href="/wiki/Commentariolus" title="Commentariolus">Commentariolus</a></i> was found in an earlier work by Ibn al-Shatir (d. c. 1375) of Damascus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swerdlow_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swerdlow-105">[87]</a></sup> Ibn al-Shatir's lunar and Mercury models are also identical to those of Copernicus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[88]</a></sup> This has led some scholars to argue that Copernicus must have had access to some yet to be identified work on the ideas of those earlier astronomers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google42_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-google42-107">[89]</a></sup> However, no likely candidate for this conjectured work has yet come to light, and other scholars have argued that Copernicus could well have developed these ideas independently of the late Islamic tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google43_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-google43-108">[90]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Copernicus cited some of the Islamic astronomers whose theories and observations he used in <i>De Revolutionibus</i>, namely <a href="/wiki/Al-Battani" title="Al-Battani">al-Battani</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thabit_ibn_Qurra" title="Thabit ibn Qurra">Thabit ibn Qurra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Zarqali" title="Al-Zarqali">al-Zarqali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Bitruji" title="Al-Bitruji">al-Bitruji</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freely_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-freely-109">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copernicus">Copernicus</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Copernicus's schematic diagram of his heliocentric theory of the Solar System from <i>De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[92]</a></sup></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="726" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg/200px-De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg/300px-De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg/400px-De_Revolutionibus_ms_p9v.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">As it appears in the surviving autograph manuscript</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="585" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg/200px-Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg/300px-Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg/400px-Copernican_heliocentrism_theory_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">As it appears in the first printed edition</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Copernicus's major work on his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> was <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">Dē revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (<i>On the Revolutions of the Celestial Spheres</i>), published in the year of his death, 1543. He had formulated his theory by 1510. "He wrote out a short overview of his new heavenly arrangement [known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Commentariolus" title="Commentariolus">Commentariolus</a></i>, or <i>Brief Sketch</i>], also probably in 1510 [but no later than May 1514], and sent it off to at least one correspondent beyond <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varmia" title="Varmia">Varmia</a> [the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "Warmia"]. That person in turn copied the document for further circulation, and presumably the new recipients did, too..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copernicus's <i>Commentariolus</i> summarized his heliocentric theory. It listed the "assumptions" upon which the theory was based, as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_assumptions_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-seven_assumptions-112">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<ol><li>There is no one center of all the celestial circles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[95]</a></sup> or spheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>The center of the earth is not the center of the universe, but only the center towards which heavy bodies move and the center of the lunar sphere.</li>
<li>All the spheres surround the sun as if it were in the middle of them all, and therefore the center of the universe is near the sun.</li>
<li>The ratio of the earth's distance from the sun to the height of the <a href="/wiki/Firmament" title="Firmament">firmament</a> (outermost celestial sphere containing the stars) is so much smaller than the ratio of the earth's radius to its distance from the sun that the distance from the earth to the sun is imperceptible in comparison with the height of the firmament.</li>
<li>Whatever motion appears in the firmament arises not from any motion of the firmament, but from the earth's motion. The earth together with its circumjacent elements performs a complete rotation on its fixed poles in a daily motion, while the firmament and highest heaven abide unchanged.</li>
<li>What appear to us as motions of the sun arise not from its motion but from the motion of the earth and our sphere, with which we revolve about the sun like any other planet. The earth has, then, more than one motion.</li>
<li>The apparent retrograde and direct motion of the planets arises not from their motion but from the earth's. The motion of the earth alone, therefore, suffices to explain so many apparent inequalities in the heavens.</li></ol></blockquote>
<p><i>De revolutionibus</i> itself was divided into six sections or parts, called "books":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>General vision of the heliocentric theory, and a summarized exposition of his idea of the World</li>
<li>Mainly theoretical, presents the principles of spherical astronomy and a list of stars (as a basis for the arguments developed in the subsequent books)</li>
<li>Mainly dedicated to the apparent motions of the Sun and to related phenomena</li>
<li>Description of the Moon and its orbital motions</li>
<li>Exposition of the motions in longitude of the non-terrestrial planets</li>
<li>Exposition of the motions in latitude of the non-terrestrial planets</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Successors">Successors</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Copernican_Revolution" title="Copernican Revolution">Copernican Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg/205px-KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg/308px-KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg/410px-KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KOS_sarkofag_ze_szcz%C4%85tkami_Kopernika.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Casket with Copernicus's remains on exhibition in <a href="/wiki/Olsztyn" title="Olsztyn">Olsztyn</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Georg Joachim Rheticus</a> could have been Copernicus's successor, but did not rise to the occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Repcheck_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-65">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Reinhold" title="Erasmus Reinhold">Erasmus Reinhold</a> could have been his successor, but died prematurely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Repcheck_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-65">[51]</a></sup> The first of the great successors was <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Repcheck_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-65">[51]</a></sup> (though he did not think the Earth orbited the Sun), followed by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Repcheck_65-5"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-65">[51]</a></sup> who had collaborated with Tycho in Prague and benefited from Tycho's decades' worth of detailed observational data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the near universal acceptance later of the heliocentric idea (though not the epicycles or the circular orbits), Copernicus's theory was originally slow to catch on. Scholars hold that sixty years after the publication of <i>The Revolutions</i> there were only around 15 astronomers espousing Copernicanism in all of Europe: "<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Digges" title="Thomas Digges">Thomas Digges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> in England; <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> in Italy; <a href="/wiki/Diego_de_Z%C3%BA%C3%B1iga" title="Diego de Zúñiga">Diego Zuniga</a> in Spain; <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> in the Low Countries; and in Germany, the largest group—<a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Georg Joachim Rheticus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Maestlin" title="Michael Maestlin">Michael Maestlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Rothmann" title="Christoph Rothmann">Christoph Rothmann</a> (who may have later recanted),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danielson_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danielson-117">[99]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danielson_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Danielson-117">[99]</a></sup> Additional possibilities are Englishman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Achilles_Gasser" title="Achilles Gasser">Achilles Gasser</a>, Georg Vogelin, <a href="/wiki/Valentin_Otto" title="Valentin Otto">Valentin Otto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danielson_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Danielson-117">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Koestler" title="Arthur Koestler">Arthur Koestler</a>, in his popular book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sleepwalkers_(Koestler_book)" title="The Sleepwalkers (Koestler book)">The Sleepwalkers</a></i>, asserted that Copernicus's book had not been widely read on its first publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reference40_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-reference40-118">[100]</a></sup> This claim was trenchantly criticised by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Rosen" title="Edward Rosen">Edward Rosen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[s]</a></sup> and has been decisively disproved by <a href="/wiki/Owen_Gingerich" title="Owen Gingerich">Owen Gingerich</a>, who examined nearly every surviving copy of the first two editions and found copious marginal notes by their owners throughout many of them. Gingerich published his conclusions in 2004 in <i>The Book Nobody Read</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeMarco_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeMarco-120">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The intellectual climate of the time "remained dominated by Aristotelian philosophy and the corresponding Ptolemaic astronomy. At that time there was no reason to accept the Copernican theory, except for its mathematical simplicity [by avoiding using the <a href="/wiki/Equant" title="Equant">equant</a> in determining planetary positions]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup> Tycho Brahe's system ("that the earth is stationary, the sun revolves about the earth, and the other planets revolve about the sun")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup> also directly competed with Copernicus's. It was only a half-century later with the work of Kepler and Galileo that any substantial evidence defending Copernicanism appeared, starting "from the time when Galileo formulated the principle of inertia...[which] helped to explain why everything would not fall off the earth if it were in motion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup> "[Not until] after <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> formulated the universal law of gravitation and the laws of mechanics [in his 1687 <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>], which unified terrestrial and celestial mechanics, was the heliocentric view generally accepted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_and_science#Copernicus" title="Catholic Church and science">Catholic Church and science</a></div>
<p>The immediate result of the 1543 publication of Copernicus's book was only mild controversy. At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> (1545–63) neither Copernicus's theory nor calendar reform (which would later use tables deduced from Copernicus's calculations) were discussed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[103]</a></sup> It has been much debated why it was not until six decades after the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus</a></i> that the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> took any official action against it, even the efforts of Tolosani going unheeded. Catholic side opposition only commenced seventy-three years later, when it was occasioned by Galileo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tolosani">Tolosani</span></h3>
<p>The first notable to move against Copernicanism was the Magister of the Holy Palace (i.e., the Catholic Church's chief <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Censor_Librorum" title="Censor Librorum">censor</a>), Dominican <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartolomeo_Spina" title="Bartolomeo Spina">Bartolomeo Spina</a>, who "expressed a desire to stamp out the Copernican doctrine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[105]</a></sup> But with Spina's death in 1546, his cause fell to his friend, the well-known theologian-astronomer, the Dominican <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Maria_Tolosani" title="Giovanni Maria Tolosani">Giovanni Maria Tolosani</a> of the Convent of St. Mark in Florence. Tolosani had written a treatise on reforming the calendar (in which astronomy would play a large role) and had attended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Lateran_Council" title="Fifth Lateran Council">Fifth Lateran Council</a> (1512–1517) to discuss the matter. He had obtained a copy of <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De Revolutionibus</a></i> in 1544. His denunciation of Copernicanism was written a year later, in 1545, in an appendix to his unpublished work, <i>On the Truth of Sacred Scripture</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._195_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._195-125">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emulating the rationalistic style of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, Tolosani sought to refute Copernicanism by philosophical argument. Copernicanism was absurd, according to Tolosani, because it was scientifically unproven and unfounded. First, Copernicus had assumed the motion of the Earth but offered no physical theory whereby one would deduce this motion. (No one realized that the investigation into Copernicanism would result in a rethinking of the entire field of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>.) Second, Tolosani charged that Copernicus's thought process was backwards. He held that Copernicus had come up with his idea and then sought phenomena that would support it, rather than observing phenomena and deducing from them the idea of what caused them. In this, Tolosani was linking Copernicus's mathematical equations with the practices of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagoreanism" title="Pythagoreanism">Pythagoreans</a> (whom <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> had made arguments against, which were later picked up by Thomas Aquinas). It was argued that mathematical numbers were a mere product of the intellect without any physical reality, and as such could not provide physical causes in the investigation of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some astronomical hypotheses at the time (such as epicycles and eccentrics) were seen as mere mathematical devices to adjust calculations of where the heavenly bodies would appear, rather than an explanation of the cause of those motions. (As Copernicus still maintained the idea of perfectly spherical orbits, he relied on epicycles.) This "saving the phenomena" was seen as proof that astronomy and mathematics could not be taken as serious means to determine physical causes. Tolosani invoked this view in his final critique of Copernicus, saying that his biggest error was that he had started with "inferior" fields of science to make pronouncements about "superior" fields. Copernicus had used mathematics and astronomy to postulate about physics and cosmology, rather than beginning with the accepted principles of physics and cosmology to determine things about astronomy and mathematics. Thus Copernicus seemed to be undermining the whole system of the philosophy of science at the time. Tolosani held that Copernicus had fallen into philosophical error because he had not been versed in physics and logic; anyone without such knowledge would make a poor astronomer and be unable to distinguish truth from falsehood. Because Copernicanism had not met the criteria for scientific truth set out by Thomas Aquinas, Tolosani held that it could only be viewed as a wild unproven theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tolosani recognized that the <a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium#Ad_lectorem" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium"><i>Ad Lectorem</i></a> preface to Copernicus's book was not actually by him. Its thesis that astronomy as a whole would never be able to make truth claims was rejected by Tolosani (though he still held that Copernicus's attempt to describe physical reality had been faulty); he found it ridiculous that <i>Ad Lectorem</i> had been included in the book (unaware that Copernicus had not authorized its inclusion). Tolosani wrote: "By means of these words [of the <i>Ad Lectorem</i>], the foolishness of this book's author is rebuked. For by a foolish effort he [Copernicus] tried to revive the weak Pythagorean opinion [that the element of fire was at the center of the Universe], long ago deservedly destroyed, since it is expressly contrary to human reason and also opposes holy writ. From this situation, there could easily arise disagreements between Catholic expositors of holy scripture and those who might wish to adhere obstinately to this false opinion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._196_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._196-129">[110]</a></sup> Tolosani declared: "Nicolaus Copernicus neither read nor understood the arguments of Aristotle the philosopher and Ptolemy the astronomer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._195_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._195-125">[106]</a></sup> Tolosani wrote that Copernicus "is expert indeed in the sciences of mathematics and astronomy, but he is very deficient in the sciences of physics and logic. Moreover, it appears that he is unskilled with regard to [the interpretation of] holy scripture, since he contradicts several of its principles, not without danger of infidelity to himself and the readers of his book. ...his arguments have no force and can very easily be taken apart. For it is stupid to contradict an opinion accepted by everyone over a very long time for the strongest reasons, unless the impugner uses more powerful and insoluble demonstrations and completely dissolves the opposed reasons. But he does not do this in the least."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._196_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._196-129">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tolosani declared that he had written against Copernicus "for the purpose of preserving the truth to the common advantage of the Holy Church."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._197_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._197-130">[111]</a></sup> Despite this, his work remained unpublished and there is no evidence that it received serious consideration. Robert Westman describes it as becoming a "dormant" viewpoint with "no audience in the Catholic world" of the late sixteenth century, but also notes that there is some evidence that it did become known to <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Caccini" title="Tommaso Caccini">Tommaso Caccini</a>, who would criticize Galileo in a sermon in December 1613.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Westman_2011,_p._197_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Westman_2011,_p._197-130">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1415" data-file-width="968" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG/170px-Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG/255px-Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG/340px-Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicolas_Copernicus_Polish_cropped.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of a mid-16th-century portrait<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[t]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Tolosani may have criticized the Copernican theory as scientifically unproven and unfounded, but the theory also conflicted with the theology of the time, as can be seen in a sample of the works of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>. In his <i>Commentary on Genesis</i> he said that "We indeed are not ignorant that the circuit of the heavens is finite, and that the earth, like a little globe, is placed in the centre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[112]</a></sup> In his commentary on Psalms 93:1 he states that "The heavens revolve daily, and, immense as is their fabric and inconceivable the rapidity of their revolutions, we experience no concussion.... How could the earth hang suspended in the air were it not upheld by God's hand? By what means could it maintain itself unmoved, while the heavens above are in constant rapid motion, did not its Divine Maker fix and establish it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[113]</a></sup> One sharp point of conflict between Copernicus's theory and the Bible concerned the story of the Battle of <a href="/wiki/Gibeon_(ancient_city)" title="Gibeon (ancient city)">Gibeon</a> in the Book of Joshua where the Hebrew forces were winning but whose opponents were likely to escape once night fell. This is averted by Joshua's prayers causing the Sun and the Moon to stand still. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> once made a remark about Copernicus, although without mentioning his name. According to Anthony Lauterbach, while eating with Martin Luther the topic of Copernicus arose during dinner on 4 June 1539 (in the same year as professor George Joachim Rheticus of the local University had been granted leave to visit him). Luther is said to have remarked "So it goes now. Whoever wants to be clever must agree with nothing others esteem. He must do something of his own. This is what <i>that fellow</i> does who wishes to turn the whole of astronomy upside down. Even in these things that are thrown into disorder I believe the Holy Scriptures, for Joshua commanded the sun to stand still and not the earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup> These remarks were made four years before the publication of <i>On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres</i> and a year before Rheticus's <i>Narratio Prima</i>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_Aurifaber_(Vimariensis)" title="Johannes Aurifaber (Vimariensis)">John Aurifaber</a>'s account of the conversation Luther calls Copernicus "that fool" rather than "that fellow", this version is viewed by historians as less reliably sourced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobe_121-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kobe-121">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Luther's collaborator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a> also took issue with Copernicanism. After receiving the first pages of <i>Narratio Prima</i> from <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Rheticus</a> himself, Melanchthon wrote to Mithobius (physician and mathematician Burkard Mithob of Feldkirch) on 16 October 1541 condemning the theory and calling for it to be repressed by governmental force, writing "certain people believe it is a marvelous achievement to extol so crazy a thing, like that Polish astronomer who makes the earth move and the sun stand still. Really, wise governments ought to repress impudence of mind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen_1995,_p._198_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen_1995,_p._198-134">[114]</a></sup> It had appeared to Rheticus that Melanchton would understand the theory and would be open to it. This was because Melanchton had taught Ptolemaic astronomy and had even recommended his friend Rheticus to an appointment to the Deanship of the Faculty of Arts &amp; Sciences at the University of Wittenberg after he had returned from studying with Copernicus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rheticus's hopes were dashed when six years after the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De Revolutionibus</a></i> Melanchthon published his <i>Initia Doctrinae Physicae</i> presenting three grounds to reject Copernicanism. These were "the evidence of the senses, the thousand-year consensus of men of science, and the authority of the Bible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-136">[116]</a></sup> Blasting the new theory Melanchthon wrote, "Out of love for novelty or in order to make a show of their cleverness, some people have argued that the earth moves. They maintain that neither the eighth sphere nor the sun moves, whereas they attribute motion to the other celestial spheres, and also place the earth among the heavenly bodies. Nor were these jokes invented recently. There is still extant Archimedes's book on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></i>; in which he reports that Aristarchus of Samos propounded the paradox that the sun stands still and the earth revolves around the sun. Even though subtle experts institute many investigations for the sake of exercising their ingenuity, nevertheless public proclamation of absurd opinions is indecent and sets a harmful example."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen_1995,_p._198_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen_1995,_p._198-134">[114]</a></sup> Melanchthon went on to cite Bible passages and then declare "Encouraged by this divine evidence, let us cherish the truth and let us not permit ourselves to be alienated from it by the tricks of those who deem it an intellectual honor to introduce confusion into the arts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen_1995,_p._198_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen_1995,_p._198-134">[114]</a></sup> In the first edition of <i>Initia Doctrinae Physicae</i>, Melanchthon even questioned Copernicus's character claiming his motivation was "either from love of novelty or from desire to appear clever", these more personal attacks were largely removed by the second edition in 1550.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-136">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3333" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/170px-Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/255px-Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg/340px-Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grabmal_Nikolaus_Kopernikus_Frauenburger_Dom_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copernicus's 2010 gravestone in Frombork Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p>Another Protestant theologian who disparaged heliocentrism on scriptural grounds was <a href="/wiki/John_Owen_(theologian)" title="John Owen (theologian)">John Owen</a>. In a passing remark in an essay on the origin of the sabbath, he characterised "the late hypothesis, fixing the sun as in the centre of the world" as being "built on fallible phenomena, and advanced by many arbitrary presumptions against evident testimonies of Scripture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Roman Catholic circles, Copernicus's book was incorporated into scholarly curricula throughout the 16th century. For example, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Salamanca" title="University of Salamanca">University of Salamanca</a> in 1561 it became one of four text books that students of astronomy could choose from, and in 1594 it was made mandatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowther_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crowther-138">[118]</a></sup> German Jesuit Nicolaus Serarius was one of the first Catholics to write against Copernicus's theory as heretical, citing the Joshua passage, in a work published in 1609–1610, and again in a book in 1612.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[119]</a></sup> In his 12 April 1615 letter to a Catholic defender of Copernicus, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Antonio_Foscarini" title="Paolo Antonio Foscarini">Paolo Antonio Foscarini</a>, Catholic Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bellarmine" title="Robert Bellarmine">Robert Bellarmine</a> condemned Copernican theory, writing "...not only the Holy Fathers, but also the modern commentaries on Genesis, the Psalms, Ecclesiastes, and Joshua, you will find all agreeing in the literal interpretation that the sun is in heaven and turns around the earth with great speed, and that the earth is very far from heaven and sits motionless at the center of the world...Nor can one answer that this is not a matter of faith, since if it is not a matter of faith 'as regards the topic,' it is a matter of faith 'as regards the speaker': and so it would be heretical to say that Abraham did not have two children and Jacob twelve, as well as to say that Christ was not born of a virgin, because both are said by the Holy Spirit through the mouth of prophets and apostles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[120]</a></sup> One year later, the Roman Inquisition prohibited Copernicus's work. Nevertheless, the Spanish Inquisition never banned the <i>De revolutionibus</i>, which continued to be taught at Salamanca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowther_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crowther-138">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingoli">Ingoli</span></h3>
<p>Perhaps the most influential opponent of the Copernican theory was <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Ingoli" title="Francesco Ingoli">Francesco Ingoli</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_priest" title="Catholic priest">Catholic priest</a>. Ingoli wrote a January 1616 essay to Galileo presenting more than twenty arguments against the Copernican theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney_2015,_p._68-69_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graney_2015,_p._68-69-141">[121]</a></sup> Though "it is not certain, it is probable that he [Ingoli] was commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a> to write an expert opinion on the controversy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72-142">[122]</a></sup> (after the Congregation of the Index's decree against Copernicanism on 5 March 1616, Ingoli was officially appointed its consultant).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72-142">[122]</a></sup> Galileo himself was of the opinion that the essay played an important role in the rejection of the theory by church authorities, writing in a later letter to Ingoli that he was concerned that people thought the theory was rejected because Ingoli was right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney_2015,_p._68-69_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graney_2015,_p._68-69-141">[121]</a></sup> Ingoli presented five physical arguments against the theory, thirteen mathematical arguments (plus a separate discussion of the sizes of stars), and four theological arguments. The physical and mathematical arguments were of uneven quality, but many of them came directly from the writings of <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>, and Ingoli repeatedly cited Brahe, the leading astronomer of the era. These included arguments about the effect of a moving Earth on the trajectory of projectiles, and about parallax and Brahe's argument that the Copernican theory required that stars be absurdly large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two of Ingoli's theological issues with the Copernican theory were "common Catholic beliefs not directly traceable to Scripture: the doctrine that hell is located at the center of Earth and is most distant from heaven; and the explicit assertion that Earth is motionless in a hymn sung on Tuesdays as part of the Liturgy of the Hours of the Divine Office prayers regularly recited by priests."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73-144">[124]</a></sup> Ingoli cited Robert Bellarmine in regards to both of these arguments, and may have been trying to convey to Galileo a sense of Bellarmine's opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[125]</a></sup> Ingoli also cited Genesis 1:14 where God places "lights in the firmament of the heavens to divide the day from the night." Ingoli did not think the central location of the Sun in the Copernican theory was compatible with it being described as one of the lights placed in the firmament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73-144">[124]</a></sup> Like previous commentators Ingoli also pointed to the passages about the Battle of Gibeon. He dismissed arguments that they should be taken metaphorically, saying "Replies which assert that Scripture speaks according to our mode of understanding are not satisfactory: both because in explaining the Sacred Writings the rule is always to preserve the literal sense, when it is possible, as it is in this case; and also because all the [Church] Fathers unanimously take this passage to mean that the Sun which was truly moving stopped at Joshua's request. An interpretation that is contrary to the unanimous consent of the Fathers is condemned by the Council of Trent, Session IV, in the decree on the edition and use of the Sacred Books. Furthermore, although the Council speaks about matters of faith and morals, nevertheless it cannot be denied that the Holy Fathers would be displeased with an interpretation of Sacred Scriptures which is contrary to their common agreement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._73-144">[124]</a></sup> However, Ingoli closed the essay by suggesting Galileo respond primarily to the better of his physical and mathematical arguments rather than to his theological arguments, writing "Let it be your choice to respond to this either entirely of in part—clearly at least to the mathematical and physical arguments, and not to all even of these, but to the more weighty ones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney_2015,_p._70_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graney_2015,_p._70-146">[126]</a></sup> When Galileo wrote a letter in reply to Ingoli years later, he in fact only addressed the mathematical and physical arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney_2015,_p._70_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graney_2015,_p._70-146">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 1616, in connection with the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a>, the Roman Catholic Church's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congregation_of_the_Index" title="Congregation of the Index">Congregation of the Index</a> issued a decree suspending <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus</a></i> until it could be "corrected," on the grounds of ensuring that Copernicanism, which it described as a "false Pythagorean doctrine, altogether contrary to the Holy Scripture," would not "creep any further to the prejudice of Catholic truth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive48_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive48-147">[127]</a></sup> The corrections consisted largely of removing or altering wording that spoke of heliocentrism as a fact, rather than a hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[128]</a></sup> The corrections were made based largely on work by Ingoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Finocchiaro_2010,_p._72-142">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo">Galileo</span></h3>
<p>On the orders of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a>, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bellarmine" title="Robert Bellarmine">Robert Bellarmine</a> gave Galileo prior notice that the decree was about to be issued, and warned him that he could not "hold or defend" the Copernican doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[u]</a></sup> The corrections to <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus</a></i>, which omitted or altered nine sentences, were issued four years later, in 1620.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newadvent_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-newadvent-150">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1633, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> was convicted of grave suspicion of <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> for "following the position of Copernicus, which is contrary to the true sense and authority of Holy Scripture",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google50_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-google50-151">[130]</a></sup> and was placed under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilliam1_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hilliam1-152">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History1_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-History1-153">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the instance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Boscovich" title="Roger Boscovich">Roger Boscovich</a>, the Catholic Church's 1758 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_Prohibited_Books" title="Index of Prohibited Books">Index of Prohibited Books</a></i> omitted the general prohibition of works defending heliocentrism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omission_of_general_ban_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-omission_of_general_ban-154">[133]</a></sup> but retained the specific prohibitions of the original uncensored versions of <i>De revolutionibus</i> and Galileo's <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>. Those prohibitions were finally dropped from the 1835 <i>Index</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omission_of_specific_ban_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-omission_of_specific_ban-155">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Languages.2C_name_and_nationality"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages,_name_and_nationality">Languages, name and nationality</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2515" data-file-width="1599" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/170px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/255px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/340px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/de:An_den_Herzog_Albrecht_von_Preussen" title="wikisource:de:An den Herzog Albrecht von Preussen">German-language letter from Copernicus</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duke_Albert_of_Prussia" title="Duke Albert of Prussia">Duke Albert of Prussia</a>, giving medical advice for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_von_Kunheim" title="George von Kunheim">George von Kunheim</a> (1541)</div></div></div>
<p>Copernicus is postulated to have spoken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, German, and Polish with equal fluency; he also spoke <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, and had some knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[w]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[x]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[y]</a></sup> The vast majority of Copernicus's extant writings are in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, the language of European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academia</a> in his lifetime.
</p><p>Arguments for German being Copernicus's native tongue are that he was born into a predominantly German-speaking urban patrician class using German, next to Latin, as language of trade and commerce in written documents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[142]</a></sup> and that, while studying <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Canon law of the Catholic Church">canon law</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> in 1496, he signed into the German <i><a href="/wiki/Nation_(university)" title="Nation (university)">natio</a></i> (<i>Natio Germanorum</i>)—a student organization which, according to its 1497 by-laws, was open to students of all kingdoms and states whose mother-tongue was German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen1995_p._127-168">[143]</a></sup> However, according to French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Koyr%C3%A9" title="Alexandre Koyré">Alexandre Koyré</a>, Copernicus's registration with the <i>Natio Germanorum</i> does not in itself imply that Copernicus considered himself German, since students from Prussia and Silesia were routinely so categorized, which carried certain privileges that made it a natural choice for German-speaking students, regardless of their ethnicity or self-identification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen1995_p._127-168">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[z]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[aa]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google21_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-google21-173">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<p>The surname <i>Kopernik</i>, <i>Copernik</i>, <i>Koppernigk</i>, in various spellings, is recorded in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> from c. 1350, apparently given to people from the village of <a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Koperniki</a> (prior to 1845 rendered <i>Kopernik</i>, <i>Copernik, Copirnik</i>, and <i>Koppirnik</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Nysa" title="Duchy of Nysa">Duchy of Nysa</a>, 10 km south of <a href="/wiki/Nysa,_Poland" title="Nysa, Poland">Nysa</a>, and now 10 km north of the Polish-Czech border. Nicolaus Copernicus's great-grandfather is recorded as having received citizenship in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> in 1386. The toponym <i>Kopernik</i> (modern <i>Koperniki</i>) has been variously tied to the Polish word for "<a href="/wiki/Dill" title="Dill">dill</a>" (<i>koper</i>) and the German word for "copper" (<i>Kupfer</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[ab]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> <i><a href="/wiki/-nik" title="-nik">-nik</a></i> (or <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a>, <i>-niki</i>) denotes a <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <a href="/wiki/Agent_noun" title="Agent noun">agent noun</a>.
</p><p>As was common in the period, the spellings of both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponym" title="Toponym">toponym</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a> vary greatly. Copernicus "was rather indifferent about <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich_p143_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich_p143-175">[147]</a></sup> During his childhood, about 1480, the name of his father (and thus of the future astronomer) was recorded in Thorn as <i>Niclas Koppernigk</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google26_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-google26-176">[148]</a></sup>
At Kraków he signed himself, in Latin, <i>Nicolaus Nicolai de Torunia</i> (Nicolaus, son of Nicolaus, of Toruń).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[ac]</a></sup> At Bologna, in 1496, he registered in the <i>Matricula Nobilissimi Germanorum Collegii, resp. Annales Clarissimae Nacionis Germanorum</i>, of the <i>Natio Germanica Bononiae</i>, as <i>Dominus Nicolaus Kopperlingk de Thorn – IX <a href="/wiki/Groschen" title="Groschen">grosseti</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google27_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-google27-179">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google28_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-google28-180">[151]</a></sup> At Padua he signed himself "Nicolaus Copernik", later "Coppernicus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich_p143_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich_p143-175">[147]</a></sup> The astronomer thus Latinized his name to <i>Coppernicus</i>, generally with two "p"s (in 23 of 31 documents studied),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikisource_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-wikisource-181">[152]</a></sup> but later in life he used a single "p". On the title page of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus" title="De revolutionibus">De revolutionibus</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheticus" title="Rheticus">Rheticus</a> published the name (in the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_case" title="Possessive case">possessive</a>, case) as "<i>Nicolai Copernici</i>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationality">Nationality</span></h3>
<p>There has been discussion of Copernicus's nationality and of whether it is meaningful to ascribe to him a nationality in the modern sense.
</p><p>Nicolaus Copernicus was born and raised in <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussia</a>, a semiautonomous and multilingual region of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_the_Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Crown of the Kingdom of Poland">Kingdom of Poland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krystyna_Poray_Goddu_2010,_p._7_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krystyna_Poray_Goddu_2010,_p._7-182">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[154]</a></sup> He was the child of German-speaking parents and grew up with German as his mother tongue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[157]</a></sup> His first <i><a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a></i> was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Krak%C3%B3w" title="University of Kraków">University of Kraków</a> in Poland. When he later studied in Italy, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a>, he joined the <i>German Nation</i>, a student organization for German-speakers of all allegiances (<a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> would not become a nation-state <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_unification" title="German unification">until 1871</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[159]</a></sup> His family stood against the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> and actively supported the city of <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Toruń</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Years%27_War_(1454%E2%80%931466)" title="Thirteen Years' War (1454–1466)">Thirteen Years' War</a>. Copernicus's father lent money to Poland's King <a href="/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon" title="Casimir IV Jagiellon">Casimir IV Jagiellon</a> to finance the war against the Teutonic Knights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[160]</a></sup> but the inhabitants of Royal Prussia also resisted the Polish crown's efforts for greater control over the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krystyna_Poray_Goddu_2010,_p._7_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krystyna_Poray_Goddu_2010,_p._7-182">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Americana" title="Encyclopedia Americana">Encyclopedia Americana</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia-190">[161]</a></sup> <i>The Concise Columbia Encyclopedia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[162]</a></sup> <i>The Oxford World Encyclopedia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia43_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia43-192">[163]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/World_Book_Encyclopedia" title="World Book Encyclopedia">World Book Encyclopedia</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copernicus1_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-copernicus1-193">[164]</a></sup> refer to Copernicus as a "Polish astronomer". Sheila Rabin, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>, describes Copernicus as a "child of a German family [who] was a subject of the Polish crown",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford-16">[10]</a></sup> while Manfred Weissenbacher writes that Copernicus's father was a Germanized Pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weissenbacher_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-weissenbacher-194">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>No Polish texts by Copernicus survive due to the rarity of Polish language in literature before the writings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_Renaissance" title="Polish Renaissance">Polish Renaissance</a> poets <a href="/wiki/Miko%C5%82aj_Rej" title="Mikołaj Rej">Mikołaj Rej</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Kochanowski" title="Jan Kochanowski">Jan Kochanowski</a> (educated Poles had generally written in Latin); but it is known that Copernicus knew Polish on a par with German and Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_Burleigh" title="Michael Burleigh">Michael Burleigh</a> describes the nationality debate as a "totally insignificant battle" between German and Polish scholars during the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Germany_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Germany-196">[167]</a></sup> Polish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Rudnicki" title="Konrad Rudnicki">Konrad Rudnicki</a> calls the discussion a "fierce scholarly quarrel in ... times of nationalism" and describes Copernicus as an inhabitant of a German-speaking territory that belonged to Poland, himself being of mixed Polish-German extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudnicki_2006_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudnicki_2006-197">[168]</a></sup>
