</p><p><a href="/wiki/Czes%C5%82aw_Mi%C5%82osz" title="Czesław Miłosz">Czesław Miłosz</a> describes the debate as an "absurd" projection of a modern understanding of nationality onto <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> people, who identified with their home territories rather than with a nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milosz37_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Milosz37-198">[169]</a></sup> Similarly, historian <a href="/wiki/Norman_Davies" title="Norman Davies">Norman Davies</a> writes that Copernicus, as was common in his era, was "largely indifferent" to nationality, being a local patriot who considered himself "<a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Prussian</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies20_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies20-199">[170]</a></sup> Miłosz and Davies both write that Copernicus had a <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-language</a> cultural background, while his working language was <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> in accord with the usage of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milosz37_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Milosz37-198">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies20_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies20-199">[170]</a></sup> Additionally, according to Davies, "there is ample evidence that he knew the Polish language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies20_199-2"><a href="#cite_note-Davies20-199">[170]</a></sup> Davies concludes that, "Taking everything into consideration, there is good reason to regard him both as a German and as a Pole: and yet, in the sense that modern nationalists understand it, he was neither."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies20_199-3"><a href="#cite_note-Davies20-199">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copernicia">Copernicia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:288px;max-width:288px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg/140px-Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg/210px-Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg/280px-Copernic_Montreal_01.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Replica of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_Monument,_Warsaw" title="Nicolaus Copernicus Monument, Warsaw">Warsaw's Copernicus Monument</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg/140px-Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg/210px-Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg/280px-Copernicus_Walhalla.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Bust by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Schadow" title="Johann Gottfried Schadow">Schadow</a>, 1807, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walhalla_memorial" title="Walhalla memorial">Walhalla memorial</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2576" data-file-width="1932" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/170px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/255px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/340px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_Monument_in_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Kraków">Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Kraków</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/220px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/330px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/440px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Copernicus outside of Frombork Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Copernicia" title="Copernicia">Copernicia</a>, a genus of palm trees native to South America and the Greater Antilles, was named after Copernicus in 1837. In some of the species, the leaves are coated with a thin layer of <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Carnauba_wax" title="Carnauba wax">carnauba wax</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copernicium">Copernicium</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copernicium" title="Copernicium">Copernicium</a></div>
<p>On 14 July 2009, the discoverers, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gesellschaft_f%C3%BCr_Schwerionenforschung" title="Gesellschaft für Schwerionenforschung">Gesellschaft für Schwerionenforschung</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>, Germany, of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> 112 (temporarily named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ununbium" title="Ununbium">ununbium</a>) proposed to the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) that its permanent name be "<a href="/wiki/Copernicium" title="Copernicium">copernicium</a>" (symbol Cn). "After we had named elements after our city and our state, we wanted to make a statement with a name that was known to everyone," said Hofmann. "We didn't want to select someone who was a German. We were looking world-wide."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fox_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-fox-200">[171]</a></sup> On the 537th anniversary of his birthday the name became official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Element112_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Element112-201">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="55_Cancri_A">55 Cancri A</span></h3>
<p>In July 2014 the International Astronomical Union launched <a href="/wiki/NameExoWorlds" title="NameExoWorlds">NameExoWorlds</a>, a process for giving proper names to certain exoplanets and their host stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[173]</a></sup> The process involved public nomination and voting for the new names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[174]</a></sup> In December 2015, the IAU announced the winning name for <a href="/wiki/55_Cancri" title="55 Cancri">55 Cancri A</a> was Copernicus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span></h3>
<p>Copernicus is commemorated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_Monument_in_Warsaw" title="Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Warsaw">Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Warsaw</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Bertel_Thorvaldsen" title="Bertel Thorvaldsen">Bertel Thorvaldsen</a> (1822), completed in 1830; and by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko" title="Jan Matejko">Jan Matejko</a>'s 1873 painting, <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Copernicus,_or_Conversations_with_God" title="Astronomer Copernicus, or Conversations with God">Astronomer Copernicus, or Conversations with God</a></i>.
</p><p>Named for Copernicus are <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_University_in_Toru%C5%84" title="Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń">Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń</a>; <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Copernicus_Science_Centre" title="Copernicus Science Centre">Copernicus Science Centre</a>; Copernicus Hospital, in Poland's third largest city, <a href="/wiki/%C5%81%C3%B3d%C5%BA" title="Łódź">Łódź</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a> International Airport (<a href="/wiki/Copernicus_Airport_Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Copernicus Airport Wrocław">Copernicus Airport Wrocław</a>).
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Copernicus_Award" title="Copernicus Award">Copernicus Award</a> has been established jointly by the <a href="/wiki/Foundation_for_Polish_Science" title="Foundation for Polish Science">Foundation for Polish Science</a> and the <a href="/wiki/German_Research_Foundation" title="German Research Foundation">German Research Foundation</a>, to promote Polish-German scientific cooperation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h3>
<p>Contemporary literary and artistic works inspired by Copernicus include:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Mover_of_the_Earth,_Stopper_of_the_Sun" title="Mover of the Earth, Stopper of the Sun">Mover of the Earth, Stopper of the Sun</a></i>, overture for symphony orchestra, by composer <a href="/wiki/Svitlana_Azarova" title="Svitlana Azarova">Svitlana Azarova</a>, commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Orchestre_national_d%27%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Orchestre national d'Île-de-France">ONDIF</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nedpho_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-nedpho-206">[177]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Copernicus" title="Doctor Copernicus">Doctor Copernicus</a></i>, 1975 novel by <a href="/wiki/John_Banville" title="John Banville">John Banville</a>, sketching the life of Copernicus and the 16th-century world in which he lived.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The oldest known portrait of Copernicus is that on the <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_astronomical_clock" title="Strasbourg astronomical clock">Strasbourg astronomical clock</a>, made by <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Stimmer" title="Tobias Stimmer">Tobias Stimmer</a> c. 1571–74. According to the inscription next to that portrait, it was made from a self-portrait by Copernicus himself. This has led to speculation that the Toruń portrait, whose provenance is unknown, may be a copy based on the same self-portrait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern pronunciation of the Polish form of the name: <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="pl-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[miˈkɔwaj kɔˈpɛrɲik]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Miko%C5%82aj_Kopernik.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Pl-Miko%C5%82aj_Kopernik.ogg" title="Pl-Mikołaj Kopernik.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-AristarchusNote01a-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AristarchusNote01a_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AristarchusNote01a_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Greek mathematician and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> proposed such a system during the third century BCE (<a href="#Reference-Dreyer-1953">Dreyer 1953</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/historyofplaneta00dreyuoft#page/134/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">pp. 135–48</a>).In an early unpublished manuscript of <i>De Revolutionibus</i> (which still survives today in the <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_Library" title="Jagiellonian Library">Jagiellonian Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>), Copernicus wrote that "It is credible that ... <a href="/wiki/Philolaus" title="Philolaus">Philolaus</a> believed in the mobility of the Earth and some even say that  Aristarchus was of that opinion", a passage that was removed from the published edition, a decision described by <a href="/wiki/Owen_Gingerich" title="Owen Gingerich">Owen Gingerich</a> as "eminently sensible" "from an editorial viewpoint".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8">[6]</a></sup> Philolaus was not a heliocentrist as he thought that both the Earth and the Sun moved around a central fire. Gingerich says that there is no evidence that Copernicus was aware of the few clear references to Aristarchus's heliocentrism in ancient texts  (as distinct from one other unclear and confusing one), especially <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sand-Reckoner" title="The Sand-Reckoner">The Sand-Reckoner</a></i> (which was not in print until the year after Copernicus died), and that it would have been in his interest to mention them had he known of them, before concluding that he developed his idea and its justification independently of Aristarchus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8">[6]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Dava_Sobel" title="Dava Sobel">Dava Sobel</a> (2011) writes:  "Copernicus had no idea that Aristarchus of Samos had proposed much the same thing [as Copernicus was contemplating by 1510, when he wrote his <i>Brief Sketch</i>, otherwise also known as the <i>Commentariolus</i>] in the third century B.C.  The only work by Aristarchus known to Copernicus—a treatise called <i>On the Sizes and Distances of the Sun and Moon</i>—made no mention of a heliocentric plan." Sobel (2011) pp. 18–19.  Sobel further writes that in Copernicus's dedication of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_the_Revolutions_of_the_Heavenly_Spheres" title="On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres">On the Revolutions</a></i> to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>—which Copernicus hoped would dampen criticism of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> by "babblers... completely ignorant of [astronomy]"—the book's author wrote that, in rereading all of philosophy, in the pages of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> he had found references to those few thinkers who dared to move the Earth "against the traditional opinion of astronomers and almost against common sense."  Sobel comments: "He still knew nothing of the Earth-moving plan of Aristarchus, which had not yet been reported to Latin audiences" (pp. 179–82).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Kish1978-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kish1978_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kish1978_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/George_Kish" title="George Kish">George Kish</a> (1978) argues that Copernicus knew about Aristarchus's heliocentric theory, saying: "Copernicus himself admitted that the theory was attributed to Aristarchus, though this does not seem to be generally known. ... it is a curious fact that Copernicus did mention the theory of Aristarchus in a passage which he later suppressed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[72]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Copernicus seems to have drawn up some notes [on the displacement of good coin from circulation by debased coin] while he was at <a href="/wiki/Olsztyn" title="Olsztyn">Olsztyn</a> in 1519. He made them the basis of a report on the matter, written in German, which he presented to the Prussian Diet held in 1522 at <a href="/wiki/Grudzi%C4%85dz" title="Grudziądz">Grudziądz</a>... He later drew up a revised and enlarged version of his little treatise, this time in Latin, and setting forth a general theory of money, for presentation to the Diet of 1528."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[8]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The name of the village, not unlike that of the astronomer's family, has been variously spelled. A large German atlas of Silesia, published by Wieland in Nuremberg in 1731, spells it Kopernik."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[13]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In 1512, Bishop Watzenrode died suddenly after attending King Sigismund's wedding feast in Kraków. Rumors abounded that the bishop had been poisoned by agents of his long-time foe, the Teutonic Knights."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[20]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[Watzenrode] was also firm, and the Teutonic Knights, who remained a constant menace, did not like him at all; the Grand Master of the order once described him as 'the devil incarnate'. [Watzenrode] was the trusted friend and advisor of three [Polish] kings in succession: John Albert, Alexander (not to be confused with the poisoning pope), and Sigismund; and his influence greatly strengthened the ties between Warmia and Poland proper."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[21]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"To obtain for his nephews [Nicolaus and Andreas] the necessary support [for their studies in Italy], the bishop [Lucas Watzenrode the Younger] procured their election as canons by the chapter of Frauenburg (1497–1498)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[25]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dobrzycki and Hajdukiewicz (1969) describe Copernicus having attended school at <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82oc%C5%82awek" title="Włocławek">Włocławek</a> as unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb4_17-16"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-17">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Translated to English, it reads: "Here, where stood the house of Domenico Maria Novara, professor of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studium_of_Bologna" title="Studium of Bologna">Studium of Bologna</a>, NICOLAUS COPERNICUS, the Polish mathematician and astronomer who would revolutionize concepts of the universe, conducted brilliant celestial observations with his teacher in 1497–1500. Placed on the 5th centenary of [Copernicus's] birth by the <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">City</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University</a>, the Academy of Sciences of the Institute of Bologna, the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Polish Academy of Sciences">Polish Academy of Sciences</a>. 1473 [–] 1973."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Copernicus's brother Andreas would, before the end of 1512, develop <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> and be forced to leave Warmia for Italy.  In November 1518 Copernicus would learn that his brother had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[36]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This was based on sketch by <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Stimmer" title="Tobias Stimmer">Tobias Stimmer</a> (c. 1570), and allegedly based on a self-portrait by Copernicus. It inspired most later Copernicus depictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lily_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-lily-61">[48]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A reference to the "Commentariolus" is contained in a library catalogue, dated 1 May 1514, of a 16th-century historian, Matthew of <a href="/wiki/Miech%C3%B3w" title="Miechów">Miechów</a>, so it must have begun circulating before that date (Koyré, <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">1973, p. 85</a>; Gingerich, <a href="#Reference-Gingerich-2004">2004, p. 32</a>). <a href="#thoren-1990">Thoren</a> (1990 p. 99) gives the length of the manuscript as 40 pages.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Koyré <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">(1973, pp. 27, 90)</a> and Rosen <a href="#Reference-Rosen-1995">(1995, pp. 64, 184)</a> take the view that Copernicus was indeed concerned about possible objections from theologians, while Lindberg and Numbers <a href="#CITEREFLindbergNumbers1986">(1986)</a> argue against it. Koestler <a href="#Reference-Koestler-1963">(1963)</a> also denies it. Indirect evidence that Copernicus was concerned about objections from theologians comes from a letter written to him by <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Osiander" title="Andreas Osiander">Andreas Osiander</a> in 1541, in which Osiander advises Copernicus to adopt a proposal by which he says "you will be able to appease the Peripatetics and theologians whose opposition you fear". <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">(Koyré, 1973, pp. 35, 90)</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="#CITEREFBell1992">Bell 1992</a>, p. 111,
"... Copernicus, on his deathbed, received the printer's proofs of his epoch-breaking <i>Dē revolutionibus orbium coelestium</i>."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Koestler-1963">Koestler 1963</a>, page 189, says the following about a letter from Canon <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a> to <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Georg Joachim Rheticus</a>:  "[…] the end came only after several months, on 24 May. In a letter to Rheticus, written a few weeks later, Giese recorded the event in a single, tragic sentence:  'For many days he had been deprived of his memory and mental vigour; he only saw his completed book at the last moment, on the day he died.'"  Koestler attributes this quotation to <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Prowe" title="Leopold Prowe">Leopold Prowe</a>, <i>Nicolaus Copernicus</i>, Berlin 1883–1884, volume 1, part 2, p. 554.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rosen <a href="#Reference-Rosen-1995">(1995, pp. 187–92)</a>, originally published in 1967 in <i>Saggi su Galileo Galilei </i>. Rosen is particularly scathing about this and other statements in <i>The Sleepwalkers</i>, which he criticizes as inaccurate.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The original painting was looted, and possibly destroyed, by the Germans in World War II during the occupation of Poland.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fantoli <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(2005, pp. 118–19)</a>; Finocchiaro <a href="#Reference-Finocchiaro-1989">(1989, pp. 148, 153)</a>. On-line copies of Finocchiaro's translations of the relevant documents, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#inqminutes" rel="nofollow">Inquisition Minutes of 25 February 1616</a> and <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#certificate" rel="nofollow">Cardinal Bellarmine's certificate of 26 May 1616</a>, have been made available by Gagné <a href="#Reference-Gagne-2005">(2005)</a>. This notice of the decree would not have prevented Galileo from discussing heliocentrism solely as a mathematical hypothesis, but a stronger <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#specinj" rel="nofollow">formal injunction</a> <a href="#Reference-Finocchiaro-1989">(Finocchiaro, 1989, pp. 147–48)</a> not to teach it "in any way whatever, either orally or in writing", allegedly issued to him by the Commissary of the Holy Office, Father Michelangelo Segizzi, would certainly have done so <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(Fantoli, 2005, pp. 119–20, 137)</a>. There has been much controversy over whether the copy of this injunction in the Vatican archives is authentic; if so, whether it was ever issued; and if so, whether it was legally valid <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(Fantoli, 2005, pp. 120–43)</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-157">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"He spoke German, Polish and Latin with equal fluency as well as Italian."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[135]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-159">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"He spoke Polish, Latin, and Greek."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[136]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"He was a linguist with a command of Polish, German and Latin, and he possessed also a knowledge of Greek rare at that period in northeastern Europe and probably had some acquaintance with Italian and Hebrew."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[137]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He used <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and German, knew enough <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> to translate the 7th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a>'s verses into Latin prose,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armitage75–77_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-armitage75–77-57">[44]</a></sup> and "there is ample evidence that he knew the <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish language</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies26_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies26-162">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Rosen" title="Edward Rosen">Edward Rosen</a> mentions that Copernicus recorded Polish farm tenants' names inconsistently, gainsaying that he was fluent in the Polish language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[139]</a></sup> (But decades after Copernicus, each of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s extant <a href="/wiki/Autograph" title="Autograph">autograph</a> signatures showed a different spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[140]</a></sup>) During his several years' studies in Italy, Copernicus presumably learned some Italian; Professor Stefan Melkowski of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_University_in_Toru%C5%84" title="Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń">Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń</a> asserts that Copernicus also spoke both German and Polish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O_historii_i_o_współczesności_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-O_historii_i_o_współczesności-165">[141]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Although great importance has frequently been ascribed to this fact, it does not imply that Copernicus considered himself to be a German. The 'nationes' of a medieval university had nothing in common with nations in the modern sense of the word. Students who were natives of Prussia and Silesia were automatically described as belonging to the Natio Germanorum. Furthmore, at Bologna, this was the 'privileged' nation; consequently, Copernicus had very good reason for inscribing himself on its register."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[144]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-172">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is important to recognize, however, that the medieval Latin concept of <i>natio</i>, or "nation", referred to the community of feudal lords both in Germany and elsewhere, not to 'the people' in the nineteenth-century democratic or nationalistic sense of the word."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[145]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These interpretations date to the dispute about Copernicus's (Polish vs. German) ethnicity, which had been open since the 1870s, and the "copper" vs. "dill" interpretations go back to the 19th century (<i>Magazin für die Literatur des Auslandes</i>, 1875, 534 f), but the dispute became virulent again in the 1960s, culminating in a controversy between E. Mosko ("copper") and S. Rospond ("dill") in 1963–64, summarized by Zygmunt Brocki, <i>"Wsrôd publikacji o etymologii nazwiska Mikotaja Kopernika</i> ["Some Publications on the Etymology of the Surname of Nicholaus Copernicus"], <i>Komunikaty mazur.-warm.</i>, 1970.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-178"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-178">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In the [enrollment] documents still in existence we find the entry: Nicolaus Nicolai de Torunia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[149]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/Copernicus" rel="nofollow">"Copernicus"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster" title="Merriam-Webster">Merriam-Webster Dictionary</a></i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Merriam-Webster+Dictionary&amp;rft.atitle=Copernicus&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.merriam-webster.com%2Fdictionary%2FCopernicus&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANicolaus+Copernicus"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Linton-2004">Linton 2004</a>, pp. 39, 119.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Owen_Gingerich" title="Owen Gingerich">Owen Gingerich</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/full/1985JHA....16...37G#:~:text=There%20is%20no%20question%20but,were%20found%20independently%20by%20Copernicus." rel="nofollow">"Did Copernicus Owe a Debt to Aristarchus?"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_for_the_History_of_Astronomy" title="Journal for the History of Astronomy">Journal for the History of Astronomy</a></i>, vol. 16, no. 1 (February 1985), pp. 37–42. "There is no question but that Aristarchus had the priority of the heliocentric idea. Yet there is no evidence that Copernicus owed him anything.(!9) As far as we can tell both the idea and its justification were found independently by Copernicus."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-gass2005-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-gass2005_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFGąssowski2005">Gąssowski, Jerzy (2005). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140708123031/http://www.astronomia.pl/historia_astronomii/index.php?id=614" rel="nofollow">"Poszukiwanie grobu Kopernika"</a> [Searching for Copernicus's Grave]. <i>astronomia.pl</i> (in Polish). Grupa Astronomia. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.astronomia.pl/historia_astronomii/index.php?id=614" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 8 July 2014<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 December</span> 2017</span>. <q>It results from the research of Dr. Jerzy Sikorski, an Olsztyn historian and an outstanding researcher of the life and work of Nicolaus Copernicus. According to Dr. Sikorski, the canon of the Frombork cathedral was buried in the immediate vicinity of this altar, which was entrusted to their care. This altar was the one who once wore the call of Saint Andrew, and now St. Cross, fourth in the right row.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=astronomia.pl&amp;rft.atitle=Poszukiwanie+grobu+Kopernika&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.aulast=G%C4%85ssowski&amp;rft.aufirst=Jerzy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.astronomia.pl%2Fhistoria_astronomii%2Findex.php%3Fid%3D614&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANicolaus+Copernicus"></span></span>

I.B.Tauris. p. 179. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-78453-138-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-78453-138-6"><bdi>978-1-78453-138-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Light+from+the+East%3A+How+the+Science+of+Medieval+Islam+Helped+to+Shape+the+Western+World&amp;rft.pages=179&amp;rft.pub=I.B.Tauris&amp;rft.date=2015-03-30&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-78453-138-6&amp;rft.aulast=Freely&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANicolaus+Copernicus"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Except for the circle labelled "V. Telluris" in the diagram from the printed edition, representing the orbital path of the Earth, and the first circle in both diagrams, representing the outer boundary of the universe, and of a presumed spherical shell of fixed stars, the numbered circles in the diagrams represent the boundaries of hypothetical spherical shells ("orbes" in Copernicus's Latin) whose motion was assumed to carry the planets and their epicycles around the Sun (Gingerich, <a href="#CITEREFGingerich2014">2014</a>, pp. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=po69BAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA36" rel="nofollow">36–38</a>; <a href="#CITEREFGingerich2016">2016</a>, pp. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kKVHDAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA34" rel="nofollow">34–35</a>).</span>
</li>

</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://galileo.rice.edu/sci/theories/copernican_system.html" rel="nofollow">The Copernican Universe from the De Revolutionibus</a></li>
